<tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">InfoboxÂ references</a></td>

<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;"></td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;"></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Titanium dioxide</b>, also known as <b>titanium(IV) oxide</b> or <b>titania</b>, is the naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> of <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, chemical formula <span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span>. When used as a <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a>, it is called <b>titanium white</b>, <b>Pigment White 6 (PW6)</b>, or <b><a href="/wiki/Colour_Index_International" title="Colour Index International">CI 77891</a></b>. Generally it is sourced from <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rutile" title="Rutile">rutile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anatase" title="Anatase">anatase</a>. It has a wide range of applications, from paint to <a href="/wiki/Sunscreen" title="Sunscreen">sunscreen</a> to <a href="/wiki/Food_coloring" title="Food coloring">food colouring</a>. When used as a food colouring, it has <a href="/wiki/E_number" title="E number">E number</a> E171. World production in 2014 exceeded 9 million metric tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Titanium dioxide occurs in nature as the well-known minerals <a href="/wiki/Rutile" title="Rutile">rutile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatase" title="Anatase">anatase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brookite" title="Brookite">brookite</a>, and additionally as two high pressure forms, a <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic_crystal_system" title="Monoclinic crystal system">monoclinic</a> <a href="/wiki/Baddeleyite" title="Baddeleyite">baddeleyite</a>-like form and an <a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic_crystal_system" title="Orthorhombic crystal system">orthorhombic</a> <a href="/wiki/Lead_dioxide" title="Lead dioxide">Î±-PbO<sub>2</sub></a>-like form, both found recently at the <a href="/wiki/N%C3%B6rdlinger_Ries" title="NÃ¶rdlinger Ries">Ries crater</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>. One of these is known as <a href="/wiki/Akaogiite" title="Akaogiite">akaogiite</a> and should be considered as an extremely rare mineral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> It is mainly sourced from <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a> ore. This is the most widespread form of titanium dioxide-bearing ore around the world. Rutile is the next most abundant and contains around 98% titanium dioxide in the ore. The metastable anatase and brookite phases convert irreversibly to the equilibrium rutile phase upon heating above temperatures in the range 600â800Â Â°C (1,112â1,472Â Â°F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Titanium dioxide has eight modifications â in addition to rutile, anatase, and brookite, three metastable phases can be produced synthetically (<a href="/wiki/Monoclinic_crystal_system" title="Monoclinic crystal system">monoclinic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetragonal_crystal_system" title="Tetragonal crystal system">tetragonal</a> and orthorombic), and five high-pressure forms (Î±-PbO<sub>2</sub>-like, baddeleyite-like, <a href="/wiki/Cotunnite" title="Cotunnite">cotunnite</a>-like, orthorhombic OI, and cubic phases) also exist:</p>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cotunnite" title="Cotunnite">cotunnite</a>-type phase was claimed by L. Dubrovinsky and co-authors to be the hardest known oxide with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vickers_hardness" title="Vickers hardness">Vickers hardness</a> of 38Â GPa and the <a href="/wiki/Bulk_modulus" title="Bulk modulus">bulk modulus</a> of 431Â GPa (i.e. close to diamond's value of 446Â GPa) at atmospheric pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du1_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-du1-18">[18]</a></sup> However, later studies came to different conclusions with much lower values for both the hardness (7â20Â GPa, which makes it softer than common oxides like corundum Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and rutile TiO<sub>2</sub>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and bulk modulus (~300Â GPa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The oxides are commercially important ores of titanium. The metal can also be mined from other minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a> or <a href="/wiki/Leucoxene" title="Leucoxene">leucoxene</a> <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>, or one of the purest forms, rutile beach sand. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_sapphire_(jewel)" title="Star sapphire (jewel)">Star sapphires</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">rubies</a> get their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asterism_(gemmology)" title="Asterism (gemmology)">asterism</a> from rutile impurities present in them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks451-3_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks451-3-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Titanium dioxide (B) is found as a <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> in magmatic rocks and hydrothermal veins, as well as weathering rims on <a href="/wiki/Perovskite" title="Perovskite">perovskite</a>. TiO<sub>2</sub> also forms <a href="/wiki/Lamella_(materials)" title="Lamella (materials)">lamellae</a> in other minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spectral lines from titanium oxide are prominent in <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">class M</a> <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, which are cool enough to allow molecules of this chemical to form.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/w/index.php?title=File:Evolution_production_dioxyde_de_titane.svg&amp;lang=en"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1322" data-file-width="1422" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Evolution_production_dioxyde_de_titane.svg/220px-Evolution_production_dioxyde_de_titane.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Evolution_production_dioxyde_de_titane.svg/330px-Evolution_production_dioxyde_de_titane.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Evolution_production_dioxyde_de_titane.svg/440px-Evolution_production_dioxyde_de_titane.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_production_dioxyde_de_titane.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Evolution of the global production of titanium dioxide according to process.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The production method depends on the feedstock. The most common method for the production of titanium dioxide utilizes the mineral <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a>. Ilmenite is mixed with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>. This reacts to remove the iron oxide group in the ilmenite. The by-product <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">iron(II) sulfate</a> is crystallized and filtered-off to yield only the titanium salt in the digestion solution. This product is called synthetic rutile. This is further processed in a similar way to rutile to give the titanium dioxide product. Synthetic rutile and titanium slags are made especially for titanium dioxide production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Production_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Production-24">[24]</a></sup> The use of ilminite ore usually only produces pigment grade titanium dioxide. Another method for the production of synthetic rutile from ilmenite utilizes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Becher_Process" title="Becher Process">Becher Process</a>.</p>
<p>Rutile is the second most abundant mineral sand. Rutile found in primary rock cannot be extracted hence the deposits containing rutile sand can be mined meaning a reduced availability to the high concentration ore. Crude titanium dioxide (in the form of rutile or synthetic rutile) is purified via converting to <a href="/wiki/Titanium_tetrachloride" title="Titanium tetrachloride">titanium tetrachloride</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Chloride_process" title="Chloride process">chloride process</a>. In this process, the crude ore (containing at least 70% TiO<sub>2</sub>) is reduced with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, oxidized with <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> to give titanium tetrachloride; i.e., carbothermal chlorination. This titanium tetrachloride is distilled, and re-oxidized in a pure <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> flame or plasma at 1500â2000Â K to give pure titanium dioxide while also regenerating chlorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Aluminium chloride is often added to the process as a <a href="/wiki/Rutile" title="Rutile">rutile</a> promotor; the product is mostly <a href="/wiki/Anatase" title="Anatase">anatase</a> in its absence. The preferred raw material for the chloride process is natural rutile because of its high titanium dioxide content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_Rutile_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_Rutile-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>One method for the production of titanium dioxide with relevance to nanotechnology is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solvothermal_Synthesis_of_titanium_dioxide" title="Solvothermal Synthesis of titanium dioxide">solvothermal Synthesis of titanium dioxide</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TiO2nanotube.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1029" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/TiO2nanotube.jpg/220px-TiO2nanotube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/TiO2nanotube.jpg/330px-TiO2nanotube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/TiO2nanotube.jpg/440px-TiO2nanotube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TiO2nanotube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Titanium oxide nanotubes, <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">SEM</a> image</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nanotubes">Nanotubes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Nanotubes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Anatase can be converted by <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_synthesis" title="Hydrothermal synthesis">hydrothermal synthesis</a> to delaminated anatase <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_nanotube" title="Inorganic nanotube">inorganic nanotubes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Titanate" title="Titanate">titanate</a> <a href="/wiki/Nanoribbon" title="Nanoribbon">nanoribbons</a> which are of potential interest as catalytic supports and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocatalyst" title="Photocatalyst">photocatalysts</a>. In the synthesis, anatase is mixed with 10Â <a href="/wiki/Concentration#Molarity" title="Concentration">M</a> <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> and heated at 130Â Â°C (266Â Â°F) for 72 hours. The reaction product is washed with dilute <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and heated at 400Â Â°C (752Â Â°F) for another 15 hours. The yield of nanotubes is quantitative and the tubes have an outer diameter of 10 to 20Â <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a> and an inner diameter of 5 to 8Â nm and have a length of 1Â <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">Î¼m</a>. A higher reaction temperature (170Â Â°C) and less reaction volume gives the corresponding nanowires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another process for synthesizing <span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> nanotubes is through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anodization" title="Anodization">anodization</a> in an electrolytic solution. When anodized in a 0.5 weight percent HF solution for 20 minutes, well-aligned titanium oxide nanotube arrays can be fabricated with an average tube diameter of 60Â <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a> and length of 250Â <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>. Based on X-ray Diffraction, nanotubes grown through anodization are amorphous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> As HF is highly corrosive and harmful chemical, NH<sub>4</sub>F is now being used as the etching agent in lieu of HF. In a typical synthesis process, a formamide based non aqueous electrolyte is produced containing 0.2M NH<sub>4</sub>F and 5 vol% of DI water. The anodization process is carried out under 25V at 20<sup>o</sup>C for 20 hours, in a two electrode electrochemical cell consisting of a highly pure and thoroughly cleaned titanium plate as the anode, a copper plate or platinum wire as the cathode and the aforesaid electrolyte.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The as prepared sample is annealed in air at 400<sup>o</sup>C to get anatase phase.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chiral_TiO2_nanofibers_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1336" data-file-width="670" height="439" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Chiral_TiO2_nanofibers_2.jpg/220px-Chiral_TiO2_nanofibers_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Chiral_TiO2_nanofibers_2.jpg/330px-Chiral_TiO2_nanofibers_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Chiral_TiO2_nanofibers_2.jpg/440px-Chiral_TiO2_nanofibers_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chiral_TiO2_nanofibers_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscopy" title="Scanning electron microscopy">SEM</a> (top) and <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">TEM</a> (bottom) images of <a href="/wiki/Optical_rotation" title="Optical rotation">chiral</a> TiO<sub>2</sub> nanofibers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiral_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-chiral-31">[31]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hollow TiO<sub>2</sub> nanofibers can be also prepared by coating <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanofiber" title="Carbon nanofiber">carbon nanofibers</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium(IV)_butoxide" title="Titanium(IV) butoxide">titanium(IV) butoxide</a> (Ti(OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>). The product is then heated at 550Â Â°C for several hours in air to remove the carbon core and form TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals. When <a href="/wiki/Optical_rotation" title="Optical rotation">chiral</a> carbon nanofibers are used as templates, the resulting TiO<sub>2</sub> fibers are also chiral, i.e., they respond differently to left and right-hand circularly polarized light. Such optical activity is common for organic, but not for inorganic molecules or nanostructures; the latter are preferred for optical applications because of their superior mechanical and thermal stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiral_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-chiral-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The most important application areas are paints and varnishes as well as paper and plastics, which account for about 80% of the world's titanium dioxide consumption. Other pigment applications such as printing inks, fibers, rubber, cosmetic products and foodstuffs account for another 8%. The rest is used in other applications, for instance the production of technical pure titanium, glass and glass ceramics, electrical ceramics, catalysts, electric conductors and chemical intermediates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> It also is in most red-coloured candy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pigment">Pigment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pigment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Titanium dioxide is the most widely used white pigment because of its brightness and very high <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a>, in which it is surpassed only by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_indices_of_refraction" title="List of indices of refraction">few other</a> materials. Approximately 4.6 million tons of pigmentary TiO<sub>2</sub> are used annually worldwide, and this number is expected to increase as utilization continues to rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nano-scaled_titania_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nano-scaled_titania-33">[33]</a></sup> When deposited as a <a href="/wiki/Thin_film" title="Thin film">thin film</a>, its refractive index and colour make it an excellent reflective optical coating for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_mirrors" title="Dielectric mirrors">dielectric mirrors</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gemstones</a> like "mystic fire <a href="/wiki/Topaz" title="Topaz">topaz</a>". TiO<sub>2</sub> is also an effective <a href="/wiki/Opacifier" title="Opacifier">opacifier</a> in powder form, where it is employed as a pigment to provide whiteness and <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">opacity</a> to products such as <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paints</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coating" title="Coating">coatings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">papers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">inks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">foods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicines</a> (i.e. pills and tablets) as well as most <a href="/wiki/Toothpaste" title="Toothpaste">toothpastes</a>. In paint, it is often referred to offhandedly as "the perfect white", "the whitest white", or other similar terms. Opacity is improved by optimal sizing of the titanium dioxide particles. Some grades of titanium based pigments as used in sparkly paints, plastics, finishes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearlescent" title="Pearlescent">pearlescent</a> cosmetics are man-made pigments whose particles have two or more layers of various oxides â often titanium dioxide, <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">alumina</a> â in order to have glittering, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iridescent" title="Iridescent">iridescent</a> and or pearlescent effects similar to crushed <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">mica</a> or <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>-based products. In addition to these effects a limited colour change is possible in certain formulations depending on how and at which angle the finished product is illuminated and the thickness of the oxide layer in the pigment particle; one or more colours appear by reflection while the other tones appear due to interference of the transparent titanium dioxide layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In some products, the layer of titanium dioxide is grown in conjunction with iron oxide by calcination of titanium salts (sulfates, chlorates) around 800Â Â°C<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> or other industrial deposition methods such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_vapour_deposition" title="Chemical vapour deposition">chemical vapour deposition</a> on substrates such as mica platelets or even <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> crystal platelets of no more than 50Â <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9Cm" title="Îm">Âµm</a> in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The iridescent effect in these titanium oxide particles (which are only partly natural) is unlike the opaque effect obtained with usual ground titanium oxide pigment obtained by mining, in which case only a certain diameter of the particle is considered and the effect is due only to scattering.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">ceramic glazes</a> titanium dioxide acts as an opacifier and seeds <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystal</a> formation.</p>
<p>Titanium dioxide has been shown statistically to increase skimmed milk's whiteness, increasing skimmed milk's sensory acceptance score.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Titanium dioxide is used to mark the white lines of some tennis courts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The exterior of the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> rocket was painted with titanium dioxide; this later allowed astronomers to determine that <a href="/wiki/J002E3" title="J002E3">J002E3</a> was the <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> stage from <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a> and not an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunscreen_and_UV_blocking_pigments_in_the_industry">Sunscreen and UV blocking pigments in the industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sunscreen and UV blocking pigments in the industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skin_care" title="Skin care">skin care</a> products, titanium dioxide is used as a pigment, sunscreen and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thickener" title="Thickener">thickener</a>. It is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Tattoo" title="Tattoo">tattoo</a> pigment and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Styptic_pencil" title="Styptic pencil">styptic pencils</a>. Titanium dioxide is produced in varying particle sizes, oil and water dispersible, and in certain grades for the cosmetic industry.</p>
<p>Titanium dioxide is found in the majority of physical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunscreens" title="Sunscreens">sunscreens</a> because of its high refractive index, its strong UV light absorbing capabilities and its resistance to discolouration under <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light. This advantage enhances its stability and ability to protect the skin from ultraviolet light. Nano-scaled (particle size of 30â40Â nm)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> titanium dioxide particles are primarily used in sun screen lotion because they scatter visible light less than titanium dioxide pigments while still providing UV protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nano-scaled_titania_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nano-scaled_titania-33">[33]</a></sup> Sunscreens designed for infants or people with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensitive_skin_type" title="Sensitive skin type">sensitive skin</a> are often based on titanium dioxide and/or <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">zinc oxide</a>, as these mineral UV blockers are believed to cause less skin irritation than other UV absorbing chemicals.</p>
<p>This pigment is used extensively in plastics and other applications not only as a white pigment or an opacifier but also for its UV resistant properties where the powder disperses the light â unlike organic UV absorbers â and reduces UV damage, due mostly to the extremely high refractive index of the particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Certain polymers used in coatings for concrete<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> or those used to impregnate concrete as a reinforcement are sometimes charged with titanium white pigment for UV shielding in the construction industry, but it only delays the oxidative photodegradation of the polymer in question, which is said to "chalk" as it flakes off due to lowered impact strength and may crumble after years of exposure in direct sunlight if UV stabilizers have not been included.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photocatalyst">Photocatalyst</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Photocatalyst">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanium_dioxide_nanofiber_spiral.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="2048" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Titanium_dioxide_nanofiber_spiral.jpg/220px-Titanium_dioxide_nanofiber_spiral.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Titanium_dioxide_nanofiber_spiral.jpg/330px-Titanium_dioxide_nanofiber_spiral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Titanium_dioxide_nanofiber_spiral.jpg/440px-Titanium_dioxide_nanofiber_spiral.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanium_dioxide_nanofiber_spiral.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
TiO<sub>2</sub> fibers and spirals</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Titanium dioxide, particularly in the anatase form, is a <a href="/wiki/Photocatalysis" title="Photocatalysis">photocatalyst</a> under ultraviolet (UV) light. It has been reported that titanium dioxide, when doped with nitrogen ions or doped with metal oxide like tungsten trioxide, is also a photocatalyst under either visible or UV light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Visible_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Visible-42">[42]</a></sup> The strong <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidative potential</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Electron_hole" title="Electron hole">positive holes</a> oxidizes <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> to create <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">hydroxyl radicals</a>. It can also oxidize oxygen or organic materials directly. Hence, in addition to its use as a pigment, titanium dioxide can be added to paints, cements, windows, tiles, or other products for its sterilizing, deodorizing and anti-fouling properties and is used as a <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. It is also used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dye-sensitized_solar_cells" title="Dye-sensitized solar cells">dye-sensitized solar cells</a>, which are a type of chemical solar cell (also known as a Graetzel cell).</p>
<p>The photocatalytic properties of titanium dioxide were discovered by <a href="/wiki/Akira_Fujishima" title="Akira Fujishima">Akira Fujishima</a> in 1967<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fujishima_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-fujishima-43">[43]</a></sup> and published in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The process on the surface of the titanium dioxide was called the <b>Honda-Fujishima effect</b> (<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E6%9C%AC%E5%A4%9A-%E8%97%A4%E5%B6%8B%E5%8A%B9%E6%9E%9C" title="ja:æ¬å¤-è¤å¶å¹æ">ja:æ¬å¤-è¤å¶å¹æ</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fujishima_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-fujishima-43">[43]</a></sup> Titanium dioxide, in <a href="/wiki/Thin_film" title="Thin film">thin film</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanoparticle" title="Nanoparticle">nanoparticle</a> form has potential for use in energy production: as a photocatalyst, it can carry out <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>; i.e., break water into hydrogen and oxygen. With the hydrogen collected, it could be used as a fuel. The efficiency of this process can be greatly improved by doping the oxide with carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Further efficiency and durability has been obtained by introducing disorder to the lattice structure of the surface layer of titanium dioxide nanocrystals, permitting infrared absorption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995 Fujishima and his group discovered the <a href="/wiki/Superhydrophilicity" title="Superhydrophilicity">superhydrophilicity</a> phenomenon for titanium dioxide coated glass exposed to sun light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fujishima_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-fujishima-43">[43]</a></sup> This resulted in the development of <a href="/wiki/Self-cleaning_glass" title="Self-cleaning glass">self-cleaning glass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-fog" title="Anti-fog">anti-fogging</a> coatings.</p>
<p>TiO<sub>2</sub> incorporated into outdoor building materials, such as paving stones in <a href="/wiki/Noxer_block" title="Noxer block">noxer blocks</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> or paints, can substantially reduce concentrations of airborne pollutants such as <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>A photocatalytic cement that uses titanium dioxide as a primary component, produced by Italcementi Group, was included in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_Magazine" title="Time Magazine">Time</a>'s Top 50 Inventions of 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Attempts have been made to photocatalytically mineralize pollutants (to convert into CO<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O) in waste water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mineralize_Pollutants_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mineralize_Pollutants-50">[50]</a></sup> TiO<sub>2</sub> offers great potential as an industrial technology for detoxification or <a href="/wiki/Environmental_remediation" title="Environmental remediation">remediation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> due to several factors:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>The process uses natural oxygen and sunlight and thus occurs under ambient conditions; it is wavelength selective and is accelerated by UV light.</li>
<li>The photocatalyst is inexpensive, readily available, non-toxic, chemically and mechanically stable, and has a high turnover.</li>
<li>The formation of photocyclized intermediate products, unlike direct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> techniques, is avoided.</li>
<li>Oxidation of the substrates to CO<sub>2</sub> is complete.</li>
<li>TiO<sub>2</sub> can be supported as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thin_films" title="Thin films">thin films</a> on suitable reactor substrates, which can be readily separated from treated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_applications">Other applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TiO2crystals.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="600" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/TiO2crystals.JPG/220px-TiO2crystals.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/TiO2crystals.JPG/330px-TiO2crystals.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/TiO2crystals.JPG/440px-TiO2crystals.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TiO2crystals.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Synthetic single crystals of TiO<sub>2</sub>, ca. 2â3 mm in size, cut from a larger plate.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Titanium dioxide in solution or <a href="/wiki/Suspension_(chemistry)" title="Suspension (chemistry)">suspension</a> can be used to cleave <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> that contains the <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">proline</a> at the site where proline is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></li>
<li>Titanium dioxide is also used as a material in the <a href="/wiki/Memristor" title="Memristor">memristor</a>, a new electronic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circuit_element" title="Circuit element">circuit element</a>. It can be employed for solar energy conversion based on <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymer</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">quantum dot</a> sensitized <a href="/wiki/Nanocrystal" title="Nanocrystal">nanocrystalline</a> TiO<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> using <a href="/wiki/Conjugated_system" title="Conjugated system">conjugated polymers</a> as solid <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>Due to the significant <a href="/wiki/Ionic_compound" title="Ionic compound">ionic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electronic</a> <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conduction</a> of TiO<sub>2</sub>, it is potent to be used as the <a href="/wiki/Mixed_conductor" title="Mixed conductor">mixed conductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPG_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPG-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>Synthetic single crystals and films of TiO<sub>2</sub> are used as a <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and also in <a href="/wiki/Distributed_Bragg_reflector" title="Distributed Bragg reflector">Bragg-stack</a> style <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_mirror" title="Dielectric mirror">dielectric mirrors</a> owing to the high <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of TiO<sub>2</sub> (2.5â2.9).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_safety">Health and safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Titanium_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Health and safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox scales.svg" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="620" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/40px-Ambox_scales.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/60px-Ambox_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/80px-Ambox_scales.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>has been nominated to be checked for its <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Discussion of this nomination can be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Titanium_dioxide" title="Talk:Titanium dioxide">talk page</a>.</span> <small><i>(March 2011)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Titanium dioxide is incompatible with strong reducing agents and strong acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OHSINC_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-OHSINC-59">[59]</a></sup> Violent or incandescent reactions occur with molten metals that are very <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electropositive" title="Electropositive">electropositive</a>, e.g. aluminium, calcium, magnesium, potassium, sodium, zinc and lithium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DANGPROP_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-DANGPROP-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Titanium dioxide accounts for 70% of the total production volume of pigments worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> It is widely used to provide whiteness and opacity to products such as paints, plastics, papers, inks, foods, and toothpastes. It is also used in cosmetic and skin care products, and it is present in almost every sunblock, where it helps protect the skin from ultraviolet light.</p>
<p>Many sunscreens use nanoparticle titanium dioxide (along with nanoparticle zinc oxide) which, despite reports of potential health risks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> is not actually absorbed through the skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Other effects of titanium dioxide nanoparticles on human health are not well understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Nevertheless, allergy to topical application has been confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Titanium dioxide dust, when inhaled, has been classified by the <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">International Agency for Research on Cancer</a> (IARC) as an <a href="/wiki/List_of_IARC_Group_2B_carcinogens" title="List of IARC Group 2B carcinogens">IARC Group 2B carcinogen</a>, meaning it is <i>possibly carcinogenic to humans</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IARC_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-IARC-66">[66]</a></sup> The findings of the IARC are based on the discovery that high concentrations of pigment-grade (powdered) and ultrafine titanium dioxide dust caused respiratory tract cancer in rats exposed by inhalation and intratracheal instillation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The series of biological events or steps that produce the rat lung cancers (e.g. particle deposition, impaired lung clearance, cell injury, fibrosis, mutations and ultimately cancer) have also been seen in people working in dusty environments. Therefore, the observations of cancer in animals were considered, by IARC, as relevant to people doing jobs with exposures to titanium dioxide dust. For example, titanium dioxide production workers may be exposed to high dust concentrations during packing, milling, site cleaning and maintenance, if there are insufficient dust control measures in place. However, the human studies conducted so far do not suggest an association between occupational exposure to titanium dioxide and an increased risk for cancer. The safety of the use of nano-particle sized titanium dioxide, which can penetrate the body and reach internal organs, has been criticized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Studies have also found that titanium dioxide nanoparticles cause inflammatory response and genetic damage in mice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The mechanism by which <span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> may cause cancer is unclear. Molecular research suggests that cell <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxicity" title="Cytotoxicity">cytotoxicity</a> due to <span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> results from the interaction between <span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> nanoparticles and the <a href="/wiki/Lysosome" title="Lysosome">lysosomal</a> <a href="/wiki/Cellular_compartment" title="Cellular compartment">compartment</a>, independently of the known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apoptotic" title="Apoptotic">apoptotic</a> signalling pathways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The body of research regarding the carcinogenicity of different particle sizes of titanium dioxide has led the US <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> to recommend two separate exposure limits. NIOSH recommends that fine <span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> particles be set at an exposure limit of 2.4Â mg/m<sup>3</sup>, while <a href="/wiki/Ultrafine_particle" title="Ultrafine particle">ultrafine</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span> be set at an exposure limit of 0.3Â mg/m<sup>3</sup>, as time-weighted average concentrations up to 10 hours a day for a 40-hour work week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> These recommendations reflect the findings in the research literature that show smaller titanium dioxide particles are more likely to pose carcinogenic risk than the larger titanium dioxide particles.</p>
<p>There is some evidence the rare disease <a href="/wiki/Yellow_nail_syndrome" title="Yellow nail syndrome">yellow nail syndrome</a> may be caused by titanium, either implanted for medical reasons or through eating various foods containing titanium dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMC3176400_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMC3176400-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dunkin%27_Donuts" title="Dunkin' Donuts">Dunkin' Donuts</a> in the United States is dropping titanium dioxide from its <a href="/wiki/Powdered_sugar" title="Powdered sugar">powdered sugar</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donut" title="Donut">donuts</a> after public pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view#Due_and_undue_weight" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view"><span title="The material near this tag may be giving undue weight to a viewpoint or idea. (April 2015)">undue weight?</span></a> <span class="metadata">â <a href="/wiki/Talk:Titanium_dioxide#undue" title="Talk:Titanium dioxide">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> However, Andrew Maynard, director of Risk Science Center at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> downplayed the supposed danger from use of titanium dioxide in food. He says that the titanium dioxide used by Dunkinâ Brands and many other food producers is not a new material, and it is not a nanomaterial either. Nanoparticles are typically smaller than 100 nanometres in diameter. Yet most of the particles in food grade titanium dioxide are much larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
